Source: 2026-06-02 U-Net: Revolutionizing Medical Image Segmentation | by Yasserelhaddar | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: U-Net: Revolutionizing Medical Image Segmentation | by Yasserelhaddar | Medium url: https://medium.com/@yasserelhaddar/u-net-revolutionizing-medical-image-segmentation-f080a1b05119 date: 2026-06-02T19:39:46.113Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Medical image analysis is a crucial component of modern healthcare, enabling doctors to diagnose and treat diseases with greater precision. From identifying tumors in brain scans to analyzing the structure of the heart, the ability to accurately segment medical images is paramount. Before 2015, medical image segmentation often relied on complex, hand-engineered features and computationally expensive methods. Then came U-Net, a revolutionary architecture that changed the game forever. This article will delve into the intricacies of U-Net, exploring its architecture, advantages, and impact on the field of medical imaging. What is U-Net and Why is it Important? U-Net, short for “Convolutional Networks for Biomedical Image Segmentation,” is a deep learning architecture specifically designed for image segmentation tasks, particularly in the biomedical field. Developed by Olaf Ronneberger, Philipp Fischer, and Thomas Brox at the University of Freiburg in 2015, its innovative design addresses the challenges of analyzing medical images, which often have limited data and require precise localization. Traditional convolutional networks excel at image classification but fall short when it comes to pinpointing the exact boundaries of objects within an image. U-Net overcomes this limitation through its unique encoder-decoder structure, which allows it to capture both contextual information and precise localization details. The importance of U-Net stems from its ability to automate and improve the accuracy of medical image segmentation. Manual segmentation is time-consuming, subjective, and prone to errors. U-Net, on the other hand, provides a faster, more objective, and more accurate alternative, enabling clinicians to make better-informed decisions and improve patient outcomes. It has found widespread application in various medical imaging modalities, including: Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI) Computed Tomography (CT) scans Ultrasound imaging Microscopy Comparison of manual segmentation vs. U-Net segmentation of a brain tumor in an MRI scan Unpacking the U-Net Architecture: Encoder and Decoder The U-Net architecture is characterized by its distinctive U-shape, which gives it its name. It comprises two main parts: the encoder (contracting path) and the decoder (expanding path), connected by a bottleneck. Let’s examine each component in detail: The Encoder (Contracting Path) The encoder, or contracting path, is responsible for capturing the contextual information within the input image. It consists of a series of convolutional layers, followed by pooling layers. Each convolutional layer learns a set of features that are relevant for identifying objects in the image. The pooling layers downsample the feature maps, reducing their spatial resolution and increasing the receptive field. This allows the network to capture information from a larger area of the image. More specifically, the encoder typically follows this pattern repeatedly: Two 3x3 convolutional layers (unpadded convolutions), each followed by a ReLU activation function. A 2x2 max pooling layer with a stride of 2 for downsampling. At each downsampling step, the number of feature channels is doubled. The Decoder (Expanding Path) The decoder, or expanding path, is responsible for precisely localizing the objects of interest. It mirrors the structure of the encoder but performs the opposite operations. Instead of downsampling, the decoder upsamples the feature maps, increasing their spatial resolution. It also uses transposed convolutions (also known as deconvolution or upconvolution) to learn to reconstruct the detailed spatial information lost during the encoding process. The decoder typically follows this pattern repeatedly: A 2x2 transposed convolution to halve the number of feature channels. Concatenation with the corresponding feature map from the encoder (skip connections). Two 3x3 convolutional layers (unpadded convolutions), each followed by a ReLU activation function. Skip Connections: The Key to Precision The skip connections are a crucial element of the U-Net architecture. They directly connect the feature maps from the encoder to the corresponding feature maps in the decoder. These connections allow the decoder to access the fine-grained details captured by the encoder, which are essential for precise localization. Without skip connections, the decoder would only have access to the highly abstracted feature maps from the bottleneck, making it difficult to reconstruct the sharp boundaries of the objects. Diagram illustrating the U-Net architecture with encoder, decoder, and skip connections Implementing U-Net with Keras/TensorFlow Let’s dive into a practical implementation of U-Net using Keras and TensorFlow. This example provides a basic U-Net model that you can adapt for your specific medical image segmentation task. This code assumes you have TensorFlow and Keras installed. You can install them using pip install tensorflow. This code defines a U-Net model using the Keras functional API. The unet function takes the input size as an argument and returns a compiled Keras model. Note that the final Conv2D layer’s activation function (‘sigmoid’ in this case) and the loss and metrics in the model.compile call should be adapted based on the nature of your segmentation problem. For multi-class segmentation, you might use ‘softmax’ activation and ‘categorical_crossentropy’ loss. Training U-Net: Data Augmentation and Loss Functions Training a U-Net model effectively requires careful consideration of data augmentation and the choice of a suitable loss function. Here’s how each plays a critical role: Data Augmentation: Overcoming Data Scarcity Medical image datasets are often limited in size due to the cost and difficulty of acquiring labeled data. To combat this data scarcity, data augmentation techniques are crucial. These techniques artificially increase the size of the training set by applying various transformations to the existing images. Common data augmentation techniques for medical images include: Rotation Translation (shifting) Scaling (zooming) Flipping (horizontal and vertical) Elastic deformations Intensity variations (brightness, contrast) The elastic deformation technique is particularly useful for medical images, as it can simulate the natural variations in tissue shape and structure. Let’s see an example of how you might implement image augmentation with TensorFlow: This example demonstrates how to implement random horizontal flips, rotations, and brightness adjustments using TensorFlow’s image processing functions. Remember to adapt these augmentations and their parameters based on the specific characteristics of your dataset. Loss Functions: Guiding the Learning Process The choice of loss function is critical for training a U-Net model effectively. The loss function quantifies the difference between the predicted segmentation and the ground truth segmentation, and the model learns to minimize this difference. Common loss functions for medical image segmentation include: Binary Cross-Entropy: Suitable for binary segmentation tasks (e.g., segmenting a single organ). Categorical Cross-Entropy: Suitable for multi-class segmentation tasks (e.g., segmenting multiple organs). Dice Loss: A region-based loss function that measures the overlap between the predicted and ground truth segmentations. It is particularly useful for segmenting small or imbalanced objects. Focal Loss: Addresses class imbalance by assigning more weight to difficult-to-classify pixels. The Dice loss is often preferred in medical image segmentation due to its robustness to class imbalance. Here’s an implementation of the Dice Loss: This code defines the Dice coefficient and Dice loss functions. The dice_coefficient function calculates the Dice coefficient between the predicted and ground truth segmentations, while the dice_loss function calculates the Dice loss, which is simply 1 minus the Dice coefficient. These functions can then be used when compiling your U-Net model. Beyond the Basics: U-Net Variations and Advancements Since its introduction, U-Net has inspired numerous variations and advancements, designed to address specific challenges or improve performance. Some notable examples include: 3D U-Net: Extends the U-Net architecture to handle 3D medical images, such as volumetric MRI or CT scans. It replaces 2D convolutional layers with 3D convolutional layers. Attention U-Net: Incorporates attention mechanisms to allow the network to focus on the most relevant features in the image, improving segmentation accuracy. U-Net++: Introduces nested and dense skip connections between the encoder and decoder, leading to improved performance and flexibility. V-Net: Similar in structure to U-Net but specifically designed for volumetric medical image segmentation. It uses residual connections and a different loss function (Dice loss) to improve performance. Illustration of 3D U-Net architecture Conclusion U-Net has revolutionized medical image segmentation by providing an accurate, efficient, and adaptable architecture. Its unique encoder-decoder structure with skip connections enables it to capture both contextual information and precise localization details, making it a powerful tool for analyzing medical images across various modalities. While the original U-Net architecture has proven remarkably effective, ongoing research continues to refine and extend its capabilities, paving the way for even more sophisticated and accurate medical image analysis in the future. Key Takeaways U-Net is a deep learning architecture specifically designed for medical image segmentation. It comprises an encoder (contracting path) and a decoder (expanding path) connected by skip connections. Skip connections are crucial for preserving fine-grained details and achieving precise localization. Data augmentation is essential for training U-Net models effectively due to limited data availability in medical imaging. The Dice loss function is often preferred for medical image segmentation due to its robustness to class imbalance. Numerous U-Net variations and advancements exist, each tailored to address specific challenges or improve performance.

Source: 2026-06-02 HNSW indexing in Vector Databases: Simple explanation and code | by Will Tai | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: HNSW indexing in Vector Databases: Simple explanation and code | by Will Tai | Medium url: https://medium.com/@wtaisen/hnsw-indexing-in-vector-databases-simple-explanation-and-code-3ef59d9c1920 date: 2026-06-02T19:39:14.045Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Introduction You may have seen the term “HNSW indexing” being used a lot in the context of vector databases. A common use case of vector databases is to do semantic search in text, where the data stored in these databases are embeddings of text, or rather “text converted into vectors  of numbers”. Generally, the embedding can be any method that does this conversion, but in the context of Retrieval Augmented Generation (RAG), it’s typically a LLM that does this step. One thing to remember: semantic search in text space === similarity search in embedding space . This means that if two pieces of text are similar in meaning, they should be “close in distance” in the vector embedding space. This “distance” is typically measured by Euclidean distance or cosine similarity. This could also hold for any other mode of data e.g. images, videos etc. I want to introduce why HNSW indexing is so useful for doing a similarity search, or approximate nearest neighbour (ANN) search, in vector embedding space. We will see later that HNSW shines when we attempt to search for vectors in the database that are most similar to a new query vector we provide. What is HNSW? Hierarchical Navigable Small World graphs (yeah, the ‘graphs’ is silent) is a method designed to enhance the efficiency of searching in high-dimensional space, often used in finding the nearest neighbours to a given data point, with approximate accuracy. We would use this in datasets that suffer the “curse of dimensionality”. If we did a brute-force approach to find the top k  closest neighbours for a given vector, we would have to do this search in O(N) time, as we need to compute the pairwise distance between the query vector and every other vector. We will see later that HNSW allows us to do this in O(log N). Let’s break down how HNSW does this. The HNSW algorithm has two main phases: construction and search. When we put vector embedding data into a database, we structure the indexes of the data such that we can later search for nearest neighbours efficiently. Construction H is for Hierarchical: Skip Lists There are levels to this stuff. HNSW indexes use a hierarchical layering of data. Imagine multiple layers of graphs stacked on top of each other. The top layer is the most sparse, and each layer gets more dense with nodes until we get to the bottom layer. If a node is on a given layer, the same node is also in the layer below it. Whenever we insert new vectors into the database, we index the data in this probabilistic way. The probability of a vector being assigned to a given layer becomes exponentially smaller as you go up the hierarchy. Here’s an analogy with toys. Imagine a giant toy box with layers of shelves. The very top shelf has only a few special toys, and each shelf below has more and more toys, all the way down to the bottom, which is super full. When we put a new toy in, we sometimes put it on the top shelf if it’s very unique, but usually, it goes on the lower shelves where there’s more space. Just like in our toy box, in a computer’s memory, we organize information in layers, so it’s easier and faster to find what we’re looking for, starting from the special top shelf down to the crowded bottom. There are various other constraints which we won’t discuss here, but I hope you can start to see how this could help our nearest neighbour search to be something logarithmic in time. Though not the same, we can draw an analogy to binary search, in the sense that both techniques are trying to narrow down search spaces through a clever structuring of the data, and thus reduce search time. NSW is for Navigable Small Worlds graphs It’s a Small World at Disneyland The principle behind Navigable Small Worlds is that every node can be reached within a small number of “hops” from any other node. They say that any 2 people on earth are separated only by “six degrees of separation” (probably). It’s a small world, after all! Now when we continue constructing the index, after we select which layer the vector goes onto, we connect this vector to other vectors that are nearest to it on that layer. This is based on the principle of maintaining efficient navigability. The definition of “nearest” can vary (e.g. based on Euclidean distance for vector data) but is crucial for ensuring that the structure supports efficient search algorithms. When adding a new node, the alg,orithm performs a greedy search in the existing structure to find the closest nodes in higher layers. This search starts from an entry point at the highest layer and progressively moves down, layer by layer, until it finds the best candidates for connections in each relevant layer. This process ensures that the new node is well integrated into the existing network, preserving the small-world properties. As more nodes are added, the network dynamically updates, always seeking to maintain efficient paths between nodes. This may involve adding or adjusting connections not just for new nodes but occasionally for existing ones as well to preserve the network’s navigability. Approximate Nearest Neighbour Search Let’s pretend our database has 1 million vectors, and we want to get the top 5 most similar vectors to a new query vector. Imagine having to do the pairwise comparison 5 million times. Yikes. This is in linear time. But we can do better, we can use the index that we have built to get this in logarithmic time! Starting the search: The ANN search begins at the top layer of the HNSW graph, which has the fewest nodes (vectors). The aim is to find a node that serves as a good entry point because it’s close to the query vector in the vector space. Gathering candidates: As the search progresses to lower layers, it’s not just about finding a single vector closest to the query. The algorithm collects a set of candidate vectors that are close to the query vector, effectively gathering a “pool” of potential nearest neighbours. Choosing the top 5 candidates: At each layer, the algorithm evaluates the distances between the query vector and the vectors in the candidate pool. If a vector is found to be closer than the current candidates, it is included in the pool, potentially replacing a farther candidate to maintain the pool size (e.g. top 5 closest vectors). This dynamic updating process ensures that only the closest vectors are retained as the search progresses. Knowing when to stop: Determining when to stop the search is one of the challenges of this algorithm. The hyperparameter ef  is used to set the size of the dynamic candidate list, which affects both accuracy and search time. The search ends when further exploration fails to find closer vectors. Code Implementation You can try all this using the faiss  library by Facebook Research. Check out a code snippet: There is a lot of implementation detail I didn’t dive deep into in this article, but I think the above captures the main ideas behind the indexing algorithm. So there you have it. HNSW indexing for vector databases.

Source: 2026-06-02 Semantic search reimagined: Hybrid dense-sparse retrieval for enterprise knowledge bases | by Khayyam H. | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Semantic search reimagined: Hybrid dense-sparse retrieval for enterprise knowledge bases | by Khayyam H. | Medium url: https://medium.com/@khayyam.h/semantic-search-reimagined-hybrid-dense-sparse-retrieval-for-enterprise-knowledge-bases-19ffe83c90c9 date: 2026-06-02T19:39:01.355Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap I recall the first time I attempted to implement “semantic search” at a large company. We had the setup everyone talks about: a vector store, a solid embedding model, and a clean interface where employees could type questions. For a handful of demo queries, it worked great. Someone asked, “How do I request parental leave?” and it directed them to the correct HR page. Then the real users arrived: One person mistyped the policy name and got unrelated documents. An engineer searched for “SOC 2 gap analysis spreadsheet” and got a list of marketing PDFs. A compliance officer typed “customer data retention India” and saw a generic FAQ, even though the actual policy was located in a different folder. It didn’t take long to see the issue. The dense search picked up meaning but missed important tokens, such as acronyms, IDs, and legal terms. Keyword search caught those details, but failed whenever someone phrased the question differently. Neither approach worked well on its own. That was the point when hybrid dense-sparse retrieval became the obvious answer. That is where hybrid dense-sparse retrieval comes in. In this article, we will unpack how hybrid retrieval works, why it matters specifically for enterprise knowledge bases, and how you can implement it in practice using tools like Chroma for the dense side, combined with BM25 or neural sparse models on the lexical side. Along the way, I will point out a few places where well-chosen diagrams make the story much easier to teach. Dense vs. sparse retrieval: a quick refresher Before combining the two approaches, it is helpful to understand how each one works independently. Sparse retrieval: BM25 and classical IR Sparse retrieval represents text as high-dimensional sparse vectors over a vocabulary. Most coordinates are zero. A few tokens are present and weighted. The dominant workhorse in production systems is BM25, a probabilistic ranking function that scores a document based on term frequency, inverse document frequency, and document length normalization. It is the default in systems like Elasticsearch and OpenSearch. Strengths in enterprise settings: Handles exact tokens well, such as product names, ticket IDs, error codes, and regulation clauses. Very fast, highly optimized, mature ecosystem. Transparent scoring, which legal and compliance teams often appreciate. Weaknesses: Vocabulary mismatch kills recall. “Parental leave” and “maternity policy” may never meet. Multilingual and code-mixed queries are painful. Cannot capture higher-level semantics like “offboarding laptop return checklist” vs. “hardware asset return procedure.” Dense retrieval: Embeddings and vector search Dense retrieval uses a neural model to map queries and documents to vectors in a continuous space. Similar meaning implies similar vectors. Vector databases, such as Chroma, FAISS, and Qdrant, can then perform approximate nearest neighbor searches. Strengths in enterprise settings: Robust to paraphrases and synonyms. Works surprisingly well for multilingual and noisy text. Can encode cross-field semantics, for example, mapping “new hire setup” to pages about onboarding. Weaknesses: Can ignore rare but legally critical tokens such as “GDPR Article 17.” Can struggle with very long documents where local phrases take precedence over global semantics. Sensitive to embedding model drift when you upgrade models without re-indexing. In other words, sparse models are token-aware but semantically shallow. Dense models are semantically rich but can sometimes be token-blind. Enterprise search needs both. What is hybrid dense-sparse retrieval? Hybrid retrieval  combines lexical signals from sparse models with semantic signals from dense models into a single ranking. The query flows through two parallel paths, and a fusion layer reconciles them. At a high level, you take: Sparse score from BM25 or a neural sparse model like SPLADE. Dense score from cosine similarity or dot product between embeddings in a vector store like Chroma. Then you fuse them to produce a final ranking. Common fusion strategies include: Weighted linear combination: score = α * dense_score + (1 — α) * sparse_score Reciprocal Rank Fusion (RRF): Rank each result list and combine based on reciprocal ranks, which is surprisingly robust. Learning to rank: Feed dense and sparse features into a learned ranker such as LambdaMART or a neural reranker. In practice, even a simple weighted combination or RRF dramatically improves robustness for enterprise queries. Dense retrieval recovers paraphrased or fuzzy matches, while sparse retrieval anchors on essential tokens and IDs. Architectural pattern for enterprise knowledge bases Let us sketch a realistic architecture for hybrid search over an enterprise knowledge base. Ingestion pipeline Document collection: Confluence spaces, SharePoint sites, Google Docs, PDF policies, Git repositories, ticketing systems. Chunking and normalization: Split long documents into semantically coherent chunks, for example, 200 to 400 tokens, with overlap for safety. Normalize text (including case folding and Unicode normalization) while preserving important entities and section titles. Sparse indexing: Index chunks with BM25 in Elasticsearch or OpenSearch. Optionally apply analyzers for language-specific stemming, token filters, and custom fields. Dense indexing in Chroma: Use a sentence transformer or LLM-based embedding model to generate dense vectors. Store vectors and metadata in Chroma. Chroma supports collections, metadata filters, and various distance metrics, which work well for multi-tenant or multi-space enterprise deployments. Metadata and security filters: Attach department, region, document type, confidentiality level, and ACL identifiers as metadata on each chunk. Ensure that both the sparse index and Chroma can filter on access control, so a user sees only the documents to which they are allowed access. Query time flow Query understanding: Light preprocessing, such as language detection or entity extraction, can be helpful. Optionally pass the query through an LLM to rewrite it into a more retrieval-friendly form. Sparse retrieval: Query BM25 with filters for access control and metadata. Retrieve the top K documents or chunks based on their scores. Dense retrieval via Chroma: Embed the query using the same embedding model used at index time. Query Chroma for nearest neighbors within the appropriate collection and metadata filters. Score normalization and fusion: Normalize BM25 and cosine scores onto a comparable scale. Combine using a chosen fusion method, such as weighted sum or reciprocal rank fusion. Post ranking and deduplication: Deduplicate chunks from the same section. Optionally, rerank with a cross encoder for the top N candidates if latency allows. Presentation and analytics: Present results with highlighted terms and snippets from both sparse and dense signals. Log query, result set, and user interactions to drive offline evaluation. When hybrid retrieval saves you in the enterprise Let’s walk through concrete query types that reveal where hybrid search shines. Acronyms and internal jargon Query: “SLA for P0 incidents in EMEA” Sparse retrieval will lock onto “SLA,” “P0,” and “EMEA,” which may appear in tables, headings, or footnotes. Dense retrieval understands that “P0 incidents” are related to “critical severity outages” and can surface a page titled “Critical incident runbook for Europe region” even if it never says “P0.” Hybrid retrieval surfaces both the exact SLA policy and related operational runbooks. Legal and compliance clauses Query: “customer data deletion requests EU timeline” Sparse retrieval is effective at identifying “deletion,” “EU,” and “timeline,” potentially surfacing the exact “data subject request timelines” clause. Dense retrieval will recognize that “customer data deletion requests” align with “right to erasure” and “data subject access requests,” and may also pull in GDPR reference documents. In a regulated environment, returning both the precise internal policy and the canonical legal references is valuable. Hybrid retrieval achieves this without the need for extensive manual curation. Engineering knowledge and tickets Query: “flaky test retry policy in CI for payments service” Sparse search finds documents that literally mention “flaky test,” “retry,” and “CI.” Dense search pulls in pages about “stabilizing tests in pipelines,” “rerun on failure,” and “payments build stability,” even if “flaky” never appears. Engineers often do not remember exact titles. Hybrid retrieval retrieves the correct runbook, along with related design documents that explain the rationale behind the policy. Advanced variants The simplest hybrid system utilizes BM25 for sparse data and a standard sentence embedding model for dense data. Over time, teams often explore more advanced ingredients. Neural sparse retrieval: SPLADE style models Neural sparse models, such as SPLADE, expand documents and queries into a richer sparse representation. Instead of raw tokens, the model predicts which vocabulary items should receive weight. This allows it to capture synonyms and latent concepts while still operating in a sparse index. In practice, SPLADE or similar models often outperform BM25 in terms of recall while remaining compatible with inverted index infrastructure. You can treat SPLADE as a “smarter BM25” branch in your hybrid setup. Multi-vector dense retrieval: ColBERT style Methods such as ColBERT represent each token in a document with a separate vector and use late interaction scoring. Instead of a single embedding per document, you get a set of vectors that preserve local information. This is particularly useful for long, legal, or technical documents where a single global embedding might obscure the entire document. For enterprise knowledge bases that include large contracts, standard operating procedures, and detailed runbooks, multi-vector models can significantly improve relevance. They are more expensive to run but can be limited to high-value collections. Rerankers In addition to the first-stage dense and sparse retrieval, many teams incorporate a cross-encoder reranker that jointly scores each query document pair among the top N candidates. This reranker enables full token-level interactions, further improving precision. You can think of your architecture as: Stage 1: Hybrid retrieval to get a diverse but relevant candidate set. Stage 2: Reranking to enforce fine-grained relevance. How hybrid retrieval powers RAG and agents So far, we have been discussing topics like those of search engineers. Let us put our LLM hat on. Most modern enterprise chatbots and copilots use some form of Retrieval Augmented Generation: Retrieve relevant chunks for a query. Feed them into the prompt. Ask the LLM to answer using that context. If your retrieval is brittle, your RAG system hallucinates or answers from the wrong document. If your retrieval is robust, your LLM feels much smarter. Hybrid dense-sparse retrieval is one of the most effective ways to strengthen this foundation: Dense retrieval ensures paraphrased questions still find the right documents. Sparse retrieval ensures that rare tokens such as contract IDs, error codes, or policy labels are preserved. Your RAG system does not care how the chunks arrived, as it only cares that the correct context is displayed in the prompt window. Similarly, in agentic systems, retrieval often appears as a tool that the agent can call upon. An internal “KnowledgeSearch” tool can also serve as a hybrid retrieval service. Agents then orchestrate multiple calls to that tool as they decompose user goals. If the underlying retrieval is hybrid, the agent benefits immediately without any change to its prompt. Practical tips for implementing hybrid retrieval Let us make this concrete for an engineering team that already uses Chroma in a RAG course or prototype. Keep Chroma for dense, add BM25 for sparse: Do not rip out your existing vector DB. Add a parallel BM25 index in Elasticsearch or OpenSearch over the same chunks. Keep document IDs aligned to facilitate merging results. Normalize scores carefully: BM25 and cosine similarity live on different scales. Start with a simple min-max or z-score normalization on held-out queries. Manually inspect a few example queries and adjust the fusion weight α until your top results “feel” balanced between lexical and semantic. Use metadata filters consistently: Whatever access control, region, or department filters you apply in Chroma should also be applied in BM25 queries. Inconsistent filters are a common source of user confusion. Log both branches separately: When you log results, record separate dense and sparse scores. This allows you to answer questions such as “Did this failure happen because dense retrieval missed a key token, or because BM25 failed on a paraphrase?” Closing thoughts Hybrid dense-sparse retrieval is not an academic garnish. In complex, multilingual, and acronym-heavy enterprise environments, it is often the difference between a search system that demonstrates well and one that survives real-world usage. Alone, dense semantic search can feel magical in the lab and fragile in production. Alone, classical BM25 can feel predictable yet shallow. Together, they become a resilient backbone for both search and LLM applications. If you are building RAG systems on top of enterprise knowledge bases or designing agentic workflows that rely on internal documents, it is worth investing in hybrid retrieval early. Every improvement in your retrieval pipeline pays dividends across all chatbots, copilots, and agents that depend on it. If this article resonated with you, it is probably because you are already wrestling with the gap between “demo quality semantic search” and “production grade enterprise systems.” Hybrid dense-sparse retrieval is one piece of that puzzle. To go deeper, you can: Explore a RAG-focused course where we build end-to-end retrieval augmented pipelines using vector databases like Chroma, and walk through evaluation and hardening of retrieval. Follow that with an Agentic system design where hybrid retrieval becomes one of the tools that multi-step agents call as they plan, reason, and act in complex workflows. Together, these two learning tracks will take you from “I can call an embedding API” to “I can design, evaluate, and deploy robust enterprise knowledge systems that combine hybrid retrieval, RAG, and agents.” That is where semantic search is truly reimagined, as a carefully engineered foundation for intelligent applications across your organization. PhD computer scientist steering Technical Content at Educative. Bridging research labs, production floors, and shaping crisp learning journeys for modern SWEs.

Source: 2026-06-02 Insights from paper: TiDB: A Raft based HTAP Database | by Hemant Gupta | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Insights from paper: TiDB: A Raft based HTAP Database | by Hemant Gupta | Medium url: https://hemantkgupta.medium.com/insights-from-paper-tidb-a-raft-based-htap-database-2ab1452bfb02 date: 2026-06-02T19:38:49.976Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap
Abstract Hybrid Transactional and Analytical Processing (HTAP) databases allow the processing of transactional and analytical queries. The challenging part is to provide a consistent view for distributed replicas. Analytical requests should efficiently read consistent and fresh data from transactional workloads at scale and with high availability. TiDB proposes extending the replicated state machine-based consensus algorithm. So TiDB is a Raft-based HTAP database. The team designed a multi-raft storage system. It consists of a row store and a column store. The row store is built using the Raft algorithm and asynchronously replicates Raft logs to learners. The learners transform row format for tuples into column format, creating a real-time updatable column store. This column store allows analytical queries to read fresh and consistent data efficiently. Also, note that the column store is isolated from the row store. The team built an SQL engine to process large-scale distributed transactions and expensive analytical queries on this storage system. Beyond that, the TiDB includes a powerful analysis engine called TiSpark. The engine helps TiDB to connect to the Hadoop ecosystem.
Introduction The old RDBMSs need to be scalable and highly available. So, at the beginning of the 2000s, internet applications preferred NoSQL systems like Google Bigtable and DynamoDB_._ NoSQL systems loosen the consistency requirements and provide high scalability. NewSQL systems like CockroachDB and Google Spanner provide the high scalability of NoSQL for OLTP read/write workloads while maintaining ACID guarantees for transactions. In addition, SQL-based Online Analytical Processing (OLAP) systems are being developed quickly. Multiple systems are very expensive to develop, deploy, and maintain, so the movement toward a single hybrid system has started. These are called HTAP systems. HTAP systems should implement scalability, high availability, and transnational consistency. They must also efficiently read the latest data to guarantee throughput and latency with additional requirements of freshness and isolation. Let’s understand these additional requirements of freshness and isolation. Freshness: It means how recent the analytical queries process data. Some HTAP solutions are based on Extraction-Transformation-Loading (ETL) processing. ETL costs several hours or days, so it cannot offer real-time analysis. The ETL phase can be replaced by streaming, but there are still data consistency and governance issues. Isolation: It refers to guaranteeing isolated performance for separate OLTP and OLAP queries. Some in-memory databases enable analytical queries to read the latest version of data from transactional processing on the same server. It cannot achieve high performance. OLTP and OLAP requests must be run on different hardware resources to guarantee isolated performance. Maintaining up-to-date replicas for OLAP requests from OLTP workloads within a single system is challenging. Another challenge is maintaining data consistency among more replicates. So we understood the problems by now. What is the solution? High availability can be achieved using well-known consensus algorithms like Paxos and Raft. If we can extend these algorithms to provide consistent replicas, then our problem is solved. TiDB does that only. The team proposes a Raft-based HTAP database. It introduces dedicated nodes called learners to the Raft consensus algorithm. The learners asynchronously replicate transactional logs from leader nodes to construct new replicas for OLAP queries. In summary, they transform the row-format tuples in the logs into column format, better suited for OLAP queries. The good part is that the latency of such replication is so short that it can guarantee data freshness for OLAP queries.
Raft-based HTAP Raft and Paxos are two famous consensus algorithms. They are the foundation of building consistent, scalable, and highly-available distributed systems. Since the Raft algorithm is designed to be easy to understand and implement, the team selected Raft. The diagram below shows the high-level ideas. Data is stored in multiple Raft groups using row format to serve transactional queries. Each group has a learner role to replicate data from the leader asynchronously. Data replicated to learners are transformed to a column-based format. The query optimizer is extended to explore physical plans accessing both row-based and column-based replicas. Let’s understand the processing in detail. A learner does not participate in leader elections, nor is it part of a quorum for log replication. Log replication from the leader to a learner is asynchronous, so the leader need not wait for success before responding to the client. The strong consistency between the leader and the learner is enforced during the read time. Learners can be deployed in separate physical resources. This allows transaction queries and analytical queries to be processed in isolated resources. Let’s now understand the challenges in the above processing. How do you build a scalable Raft storage system to support highly concurrent read/write? How do you synchronize logs with learners with low latency to keep data fresh? How do you efficiently process both transactional and analytical queries with guaranteed performance? We will explore the solution of these challenges in the architecture section.
Architecture TiDB architecture The diagram above shows the high-level architecture of TiDB. TiDB supports the MySQL protocol and is accessible by MySQL-compatible clients. TiDB has three core components: a distributed storage layer, a Placement Driver, and a computation engine layer. The distributed storage layer consists of a row store (TiKV) and a columnar store (TiFlash). TiKV is an ordered key-value map. The key comprises the table and row IDs; the value is the actual row data. Using a range partition strategy, the large key-value map is split into many contiguous ranges, each called a Region. Each Region has multiple replicas for high availability. The Raft consensus algorithm maintains consistency among replicas for each Region. The leaders of different Raft groups asynchronously replicate data from TiKV to TiFlash. TiKV and TiFlash can be deployed in separate physical resources. Placement Driver (PD) is responsible for managing Regions. It also supplies each key’s Region and physical location. It automatically moves Regions to balance workloads. PD provides strictly increasing and globally unique timestamps. These timestamps also serve as our transaction IDs. PD may contain multiple members and has no persistent state. On startup, a PD member gathers all necessary data from other members and TiKV nodes. TiDB’s computation engine layer is stateless and scalable. The SQL engine has a cost-based query optimizer and a distributed query executor. TiDB implements a two-phase commit (2PC) protocol based on Google Percolator to support transactional processing. TiDB also integrates with Spark. It is helpful to integrate data stored in TiDB and HDFS.
Multi-Raft Storage The architecture of multi-Raft storage The diagram above shows the architecture of the distributed storage layer in TiDB. Here, multiple Raft groups manage data, so it is called multi-Raft storage. 5.1 Row-based Storage (TiKV) A TiKV deployment consists of many TiKV servers. Regions are replicated between TiKV servers using Raft. Each TiKV server can be either a Raft leader or a Raft follower for different Regions. TiDB uses RocksDB to persist all the data. The Raft leader handles read/write requests for the corresponding Region. Let’s look into the basic Raft process before we proceed: A Region leader receives a request from the SQL engine layer. The leader appends the request to its log. The leader sends the new log entries to its followers. The follower appends the entries to their logs. The leader waits for its followers to respond. If a quorum of nodes responds successfully, the leader commits the request and applies it locally. The leader sends the result to the client. The above process is good from a data consistency and high availability perspective, but performance could be better because the steps are sequential, and there may be a high IO cost. 5.1.1 Optimization between Leaders and Followers The second and third steps can run in parallel. If appending logs fail on the leader but a quorum of the followers successfully appends the logs, the logs can still be committed. After sending the logs, the leader does not have to wait for the followers to respond. It can send further logs with the predicted log index. If errors occur, the leader adjusts the log index and resends the replication requests. A different thread can handle the leader applying committed log entries (step four) asynchronously. 5.1.2 Accelerating Read Requests from Clients The data is read from leaders in a linearizable manner. This means that after a value is read at a time t from a region leader, the leader must not return a previous version of the value for any read requests after time t. Raft guarantees that once the leader successfully writes its data, the leader can respond to any read requests without synchronizing logs across servers. At this point, the quorum is established. The important point is that the leader role for a region may have been moved to another TiKV server after the leader election. TiKV has the following two read optimizations. Read index: When a leader responds to a read request, it records the current commit index as a local read index**.** After that, it sendsheartbeat messages to followers to confirm its leadership role. The leader can return value only if he finds himself the leader. Lease read: The leader and followers agree on a lease period, during which followers do not issue leader election requests so that the leader is not changed. In this lease period, the leader can respond to any read request without connecting to its followers. This approach works well if the CPU clock is on each node does not differ very much. 5.1.3 Managing Massive Regions To balance Regions across servers, the Placement Driver (PD) schedules Regions with constraints on the number and location of replicas. One critical constraint is to place at least three replicas of a Region on different TiKV instances. Depending on how busy the Regions’ workloads are, TiDB can adjust the frequency of heartbeats sent. 5.1.4 Dynamic Region Split and Merge A large Region may become too hot to be read or written in a reasonable time. Hot or large Regions should be split into smaller ones to distribute the workload better. Also, many Regions may be small and seldom accessed, so they need to be merged. Only adjacent Regions are merged in the key space to maintain the order between Regions. A split operation divides a Region into several new, smaller Regions. Each new region covers a continuous range of keys in the original Region. The new Region that covers the rightmost range reuses the Raft group of the original Region. The split process is like an update request in Raft: PD issues a split command to the leader of a Region. The leader transforms the command into a log and replicates it to all its follower nodes. Once a quorum replicates the log, the leader commits the split command. The command is applied atomically to all the nodes in the Raft group. For each replica of a split Region, a Raft state machine is created, forming a new Raft group. The merge process is the reverse of the above. PD moves replicas of the two adjacent Regions to colocate them on separate servers. After that, two Regions are merged locally on each server. It has two phases. First, stop the service for one Region, and second, merge it with another. 5.2 Column-based Storage (TiFlash) We have seen that TiFlash is composed of learner nodes. They receive Raft logs from Raft groups and transform row-format tuples into column-format data. Each table in TiFlash is divided into many partitions. Each partition covers a contiguous range of tuples. During initializing a TiFlash instance, if there is too much data for fast synchronization, the leader sends a snapshot of its data. After that, the TiFlash instance begins listening for updates from the Raft groups. 5.2.1 Log Replayer The logs received by learner nodes are linearizable. They are re-played in FiFO order to maintain the linearizability. The log replay has three steps: Compacting logs: The transactional logs are classified into three statuses: prewritten, committed, or rollbacked. The compact process deletes invalid prewritten logs according to rollbacked logs and puts only valid logs into a buffer. Decoding tuples: The logs in the buffer are decoded into row-format tuples. Then, the decoded tuples are put into a row buffer. Transforming data format: The buffer’s size and time duration are checked, and based on that, data is transformed to columnar data and written to a local partition data pool. 5.2.2 Schema Synchronization Learner nodes have to be aware of the newest schema for transformation. The newest schema information is stored in TiKV. Each learner node maintains a schema cache, which is synchronized with TiKV’s schema through a schema synced. TiDB has a two-stage strategy to manage the trade-off between the frequency of schema synchronization and the number of schema mismatches. Regular synchronization: The schema syncer fetches the newest schema from TiKV periodically. Compulsive synchronization: If the schema syncer detects a mismatched schema, it proactively fetches the newest schema from TiKV. 5.2.3 Columnar Delta Tree TiDB team designed a new columnar storage engine called DeltaTree. It appends delta updates immediately and later merges them with the previously stable version for each partition. The delta updates and the stable data are stored separately in the DeltaTree as shown in the below diagram. The columnar delta tree In the stable space, partition data is stored as chunks, each covering a smaller range of the partition’s tuples. These row-format tuples are stored column by column. Deltas are directly appended into the delta space in the order TiKV generates them to maintain linearizability. The store format of columnar data in TiFlash is similar to Parquet. TiFlash compresses data files using the LZ4 compression to save their disk size. 5.2.4 Read Process The learner nodes provide snapshot isolation so data can be read from TiFlash at a specific timestamp. The learner sends a read index request to its leaders upon receiving a read request. The leaders send the referred logs to the learner, and the learner replays and stores the logs. Once the logs are written into DeltaTree, the specific data from DeltaTree is ready to respond to the read request.
HTAP Engines TiDB provides an SQL engine to evaluate transactional and analytical queries. The engine adapts the Google Percolator model to implement optimistic and pessimistic locking in a distributed cluster. 6.1 Transactional Processing TiDB provides ACID transactions with snapshot-isolation (SI) or repeatable read (RR) semantics. SI helps in reading the consistent version of the data in a transaction. RR helps allow the consistent reading of data by different statements in a transaction. TiDB uses MVCC, which avoids read-write locking and protects against write-write conflicts. Let’s understand the collaboration of different components for a transaction. SQL engine: It coordinates transactions. It receives clients’ write and read requests, transforms data into a key-value format, and writes the transactions to TiKV using two-phase commit (2PC). PD: It manages logical Regions and physical locations. It provides global, strictly-increasing timestamps. TiKV: It provides distributed transaction interfaces, implements MVCC, and persists data to disk. Now, let’s understand the optimistic and pessimistic locking. The process of optimistic and pessimistic transaction The process of an optimistic transaction: SQL engine asks PD for a timestamp to use as the transaction’s start timestamp (start ts) after getting the “begin” command from the client. The engine executes SQL DMLs by reading data from TiKV and writing to local memory. TiKV supplies data with the most recent commit timestamp (commit ts) before the transaction’s start ts. When the client issues the “commit” command, the engine starts the 2PC protocol. It randomly chooses a primary key, locks all keys in parallel, and sends prewrites to TiKV nodes. If all prewrites succeed, the SQL engine asks PD for a timestamp for the transaction’s commit ts and sends a commit command to TiKV. TiKV commits the primary key and successfully responds to the SQL engine. The engine returns success to the client. The engine commits secondary keys and clears locks asynchronously. For pessimistic transactions, users can opt for the read committed (RC) isolation level. 6.2 Analytical Processing 6.2.1 Query Optimization in SQL Engine TiDB implements a query optimizer with two phases of query optimization: Rule-based optimization (RBO) of the query, which produces a logical plan Cost-based optimization (CBO) transforms a logical plan into a physical plan. RBO has a rich set of transformation rules, including cropping unneeded columns, eliminating projection, pushing down predicates, deriving predicates, constant folding, eliminating “group by” or outer joins, and unnesting subqueries. CBO chooses the cheapest plan from candidate plans according to execution costs. TiDB implements scalable indexes to work in a distributed environment. Regions split indexes in the same way as data and store them in TiKV as key values. Using an index requires a binary search to locate the Regions that contain relevant parts of the index. 6.2.2 TiSpark The interaction of TiSpark and TiDB The diagram above shows how TiSpark integrates with TiDB. Inside TiSpark, the Spark driver reads metadata from TiKV to construct a Spark catalog, including table schemas and index information. The Spark driver asks PD for timestamps to read MVCC data from TiKV to ensure it gets a consistent snapshot of the database. TiSpark can simultaneously read from multiple TiDB Regions and get index data from the storage layer in parallel. 6.3 Isolation and Coordination Analytical queries often consume high CPU, memory, and I/O bandwidth resources. Isolating these resources makes sense for better performance. Data is consistent across TiKV and TiFlash, so queries can be served by reading from either.
Related Work Evolving from an existing database Oracle introduced the Database In-Memory option in 2014 as the industry’s first dual-format, in-memory RDBMS. SQL Server integrates two specialized storage engines into its core: the Apollo column storage engine for analytical workloads and the Hekaton in-memory engine for transactional workloads. SAP HANA supports efficiently evaluating separate OLAP and OLTP queries and uses different data organizations for each. Transforming an open-source system Wildfire builds an HTAP engine based on Spark. SnappyData presents a unified platform for OLTP, OLAP, and stream analytics. Building from scratch MemSQL (SingleStore) has an engine for scalable in-memory OLTP and fast analytical queries. HyPer used the operating system’s fork system call to provide snapshot isolation for analytical workloads. ScyPer extends HyPer to evaluate analytical queries on remote replicas at scale. BatchDB is an in-memory database engine designed for HTAP workloads. The lineage-based data store (L-Store) combines real-time analytical and transactional query processing within a single unified engine by introducing an update-friendly, lineage-based storage architecture. Peloton is a self-driving SQL database management system. It attempts to adapt data origination for HTAP workloads at run time. Cockroach DB is a distributed SQL database that offers high availability, data consistency, scalability, and isolation.
Conclusion The team has presented a production-ready HTAP database TiDB. TiDB is built on top of TiKV, a distributed, row-based store that uses the Raft algorithm. TiDB has internally TiFlash as a columnar database. Log replication between TiKV and TiFlash provides real-time data consistency with little overhead. References Google Spanner paper post Google BigTable paper post Raft paper post Amazon DynamoDB paper post Wildfire: Concurrent Blazing Data Ingest and Analytics HyPer: A Hybrid OLTP&OLAP Main Memory Database system based on virtual memory snapshots Oracle Database In-Memory: A dual format in-memory database BatchDB: Efficient Isolated Execution of Hybrid OLTP+OLAP Workloads for Interactive Applications SnappyData: A Unified Cluster for Streaming, Transactions, and Interactive Analytics ScyPer: A Hybrid OLTP&OLAP Distributed Main Memory Database System for Scalable Real-Time Analytics L-Store: A Real-time OLTP and OLAP System https://www.linkedin.com/in/hkgupta/ Working on the challenge to create insights for 100 software eng papers. #100PapersChallenge, AVP Engineering at CoinDCX
