Source: 2026-06-02 A Deep Dive into Recommendation and Search for E-Commerce | by Gaurav Kumar | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: A Deep Dive into Recommendation and Search for E-Commerce | by Gaurav Kumar | Medium url: https://medium.com/@gaurav.kumar.sah/a-deep-dive-into-recommendation-and-search-for-e-commerce-5b2b2e253c20 date: 2026-06-02T19:38:38.377Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap October 2025 Section 1: A Deep Dive into Recommendation and Search Part 1 of the E-commerce Data Science Series Date: October 26, 2025 Abstract Modern e-commerce platforms must solve two distinct but related AI problems at a massive scale: Personalized Recommendation and Search Ranking. This report provides a comprehensive technical blueprint for both systems, presenting them as separate, parallel architectures. Section 1: The Recommendation System details the architecture for implicit user intent (e.g., “Recommended for You”). It covers the industry-standard, two-stage funnel: (1) Candidate Generation via a Two-Tower Deep Learning Model, an architecture famously detailed by YouTube (Google), and served with an Approximate Nearest Neighbor (ANN) index like Meta’s Faiss; and (2) Ranking via deep models like Google’s Wide & Deep or Alibaba’s Deep Interest Network (DIN). This section also includes a detailed breakdown of Context-Aware, In-Session Recommendation, moving from RNNs to Transformer-based models to capture dynamic, short-term user intent, a technique crucial for platforms like Netflix and Alibaba. Section 2: The Search System details the architecture for explicit user intent (e.g., the search bar). It also covers a two-stage funnel: (1) Retrieval via a Hybrid Search model, combining traditional BM25 with semantic vector search pioneered by companies like Amazon; and (2) Re-Ranking via a Learning to Rank (LTR) model, typically a LambdaMART GBDT. The article provides in-depth explanations of the distinct feature engineering, model architectures, loss functions, and serving infrastructure for each system. It concludes with a unified overview of the MLOps and evaluation frameworks required to deploy, measure, and maintain these critical, revenue-driving AI platforms, drawing examples from Uber’s Michelangelo, and Netflix’s experimentation culture. 1.0 Introduction: The Twin Problems of E-commerce AI Every large-scale e-commerce platform faces a dual challenge: The “Discovery” Problem (Recommendation): How do you show a user relevant products when they haven’t told you what they’re looking for? This is a problem of implicit intent. The user is browsing, and your job is to be a perfect personal shopper, anticipating their needs. The “Fulfillment” Problem (Search): How do you show a user the most relevant product when they have told you exactly what they’re looking for (e.g., “men’s blue running shoes”)? This is a problem of explicit intent. The user is on a mission, and your job is to be a perfect librarian, retrieving the exact item. Trying to solve both with a single model is ineffective. The signals, architectures, and objectives are different. A “click” on a recommended item on the homepage means something different from a “click” on the #1 search result. This report will dissect the architecture for each system separately. Both systems, however, share a common high-level pattern born of necessity: The Two-Stage Funnel. At a scale of 50 million users and 10 million products, it is computationally impossible to score every product for every user in the 200ms it takes to load a webpage. Therefore, both systems follow this pattern: Stage 1: Retrieval/Candidate Generation: A high-speed, high-recall system that scans all millions of items and selects a few hundred (~500–1000) plausible candidates. This stage prioritizes speed and recall. Stage 2: Ranking/Re-Ranking: A complex, computationally “heavy,” high-precision model that takes the small list of candidates and scores/sorts them. This stage prioritizes accuracy and relevance. 2.0 The E-commerce Data & Feature Framework Before we build any models, we must create the fuel. Your AI system is a reflection of your data. This data processing framework is foundational to both the recommendation and search systems. 2.1 The Data Triad All data falls into three categories: User Data: user_id, demographics (age, location), computed features (favorite_category, avg_purchase_price, RFM score). Item Data: item_id, content features (category, brand, price), and embedded features (from product images and descriptions). Interaction Data: The ground truth. A time-series log of (user_id, item_id, session_id, timestamp, interaction_type). The interaction_type (click, add-to-cart, purchase, view, dismiss) is our label. 2.2 Data Challenges: Sparsity and Bias This data is not clean. It is riddled with challenges that must be addressed. Sparsity: The user-item interaction matrix is extremely sparse. >99.9% of (user, item) pairs have no interaction. Position Bias: Users are far more likely to click the #1 item in a list simply because it is #1. This “clicked” signal is heavily biased. Popularity Bias: Popular items get more clicks, which get them recommended more, which gets them more clicks. This creates a feedback loop that starves new or niche items. 2.3 The Bedrock: Feature Engineering & Embeddings Deep learning models require numerical vectors. The process of converting our “Data Triad” into these vectors is the most important step. In modern DL, this is done via embeddings: learned, dense vectors that represent categorical concepts. Categorical Features (user_id, item_id, brand): Method: An Embedding Table. This is a giant lookup matrix of shape (N, D), where N is the vocabulary size (e.g., 50 million users) and D is the embedding dimension (e.g., 64). Challenge (High Cardinality): For 500M users, you can’t have a 500M-row table. You use the “hashing trick”: a fast hash function maps the user_id to a fixed-size bucket (e.g., 1M buckets). This trades memory for (acceptable) collisions. Numerical Features (price, avg_rating): Method 1 (Normalization): z-score = (x — mean) / std_dev. Simple, but assumes a normal distribution. Method 2 (Binning/Quantization): This is often more powerful. You bin price into 10 quantiles (deciles). Now, “Price_Bin_3” is a categorical feature that gets its own embedding. This allows the model to learn non-linear relationships, e.g., “users love items in Price_Bin_1 (cheap) and Price_Bin_9 (luxury) but hate Price_Bin_5 (mediocre).” Text Features (product_description, review_text): Classic Method: TF-IDF or Bag-of-Words (BoW). Fast, but ignores word order and semantics. Static Embedding Method: word2vec or fastText. You pre-train word2vec on all your product descriptions. To get an embedding for a product, you take the average of the word2vec vectors for all words in its description. This is a very strong baseline. Contextual Embedding Method (SOTA): BERT or Sentence-Transformer (S-BERT). You pass the raw text through a pre-trained S-BERT to get a single, 768-dimension semantic vector. This vector understands context and meaning (“running shoes” is close to “sneakers”). Image Features (product_image): Method: Pre-trained CNN. You take a pre-trained ResNet50 or EfficientNet (trained on ImageNet), remove its final classification layer, and pass your product image through it. The output of the second-to-last layer is a 2048-dimension visual embedding that captures color, shape, and texture. 3.0 Section 1: The Recommendation System (The “Discovery” Problem) Objective: To personalize the user experience and drive discovery by surfacing relevant items based on implicit intent (user profile, context, and past behavior). 3.1 Defining the Recommendation Funnel Business Goal: Increase engagement and sales (CTR, CVR, AOV) on surfaces like the homepage “Recommended for You,” product-page carousels “Similar Items,” and email campaigns. Core Metrics: Offline (Historical Data): Recall@K (for Stage 1), AUC (Area Under Curve) (for Stage 2). Online (Live A/B Test): CTR, CVR, Revenue Per User. 3.2 Reco Stage 1: Candidate Generation via Two-Tower Model This is the most scalable architecture for recommendation retrieval. It consists of two independent neural networks. The User Tower: Compresses all user information into one vector. Inputs: user_id embedding, location embedding, avg_purchase_price, and an embedding of the user’s history (e.g., the average of the embeddings of the last 20 items they clicked). Output: A single User Embedding (e.g., 128 dimensions). The Item Tower: Compresses all item information into one vector of the same dimension. Inputs: item_id embedding, category embedding, price embedding, and the pre-computed text and image embeddings. Output: A single Item Embedding (e.g., 128 dimensions). 3.2.1 Company Deep Dive: YouTube’s 2016 Recommendation Paper The Two-Tower model was famously detailed by Google in their 2016 paper, “Deep Neural Networks for YouTube Recommendations.” This paper is arguably the most influential modern recommendation paper. YouTube’s Implementation: User Tower: Their “user” features included embeddings of watch history, search hist,ory, demographic features, and a critical feature: “example age” (the age of the training example). This “example age” feature helped the model correct for the fact that new videos naturally have lower CTRs and prevented the model from being biased toward old, popular content. Item Tower: Their “video” features included the video ID embedding, category, etc. Objective: They did not predict Click-Through Rate (CTR). They predicted Watch Time per Impression. This was a brilliant business decision. Optimizing for CTR leads to clickbait. Optimizing for watch time leads to engagement and satisfaction. Serving: At serving time, they used an ANN-like system to find the “Top N” videos closest to the user’s vector, which were then fed to a separate ranker. This paper proved the two-stage funnel’s effectiveness at a massive scale. 3.2.2 Training & Loss Functions During training, the (user, item) towers are brought together. The goal is to make the dot product similarity high for positive pairs and low for negative pairs. Negative Sampling: This is critical. In-Batch Negatives (Standard): For a training batch of 1024 (user, item) pairs, each user_vector is paired with its pos_item_vector. It treats the other 1023 item_vectors in the batch as negatives. This is extremely efficient. Hard Negative Mining (Advanced): You explicitly find items the model incorrectly scores as high for a user. This teaches the model to distinguish between “Pro running shoe” (clicked) and “Basic running shoe” (ignored). 3.2.3 Serving: The ANN Revolution The independent tower design allows for a brilliant serving strategy: Offline (Batch Job): Once per day, the Item Tower is used to pre-compute the 128-dim vector for all 10 million products. Indexing: These 10M vectors are loaded into an Approximate Nearest Neighbor (ANN) Vector Index. Online (Real-time): When a user visits, their data is fed through the User Tower to get one user_vector. This vector is used to query the ANN index, which returns the Top 500 “closest” item vectors in < 50ms. 3.2.4 Company Deep Dive: Meta (Faiss) & Google (ScaNN) This real-time retrieval is only possible because of ANN libraries. Meta (Faiss): Facebook (Meta) open-sourced Faiss (Facebook AI Similarity Search). It’s an incredibly fast library that uses techniques like Product Quantization to compress the vectors, allowing them to fit in RAM, and Inverted File Indexes (IVF) to partition the search space. It trades 100% perfect accuracy (Exact KNN) for 1000x speed. Google (ScaNN): Google open-sourced ScaNN (Scalable Nearest Neighbors), which is the library that powers many of their own vector search products. It uses advanced techniques like Anisotropic Vector Quantization and is known for its high performance. 3.3 Reco Stage 2: The Ranking (Scoring) The 500 candidates from Stage 1 are relevant, but they are not optimally ranked. The Two-Tower model has a key weakness: it never learns the explicit interaction between a user feature and an item feature (e.g., user_location=’New York’ and item_category=’Winter Coat’). The Ranker is a cross-network that can learn these features. It takes a combined feature vector for each (user, candidate_item) pair and outputs a single score (e.g., pCTR). 3.3.1 Core Architecture 1: Google’s “Wide & Deep Learning” In 2016, Google published “Wide & Deep Learning for Recommender Systems,” introducing a model that was simultaneously optimized for memorization and generalization. Google’s Wide & Deep: The Wide Part (Memorization): This is a simple, linear model (like logistic regression). Its job is to “memorize” simple, direct rules. It is fed sparse, crossed features. Feature Cross: A new feature created by “crossing” two or more categorical features. E.g., AND(user_location=’NY’, item_category=’Coat’). This single feature is a very strong signal. The Deep Part (Generalization): This is a standard DNN. Its job is to find complex, non-linear, “unseen” patterns from the dense embeddings (e.g., “this type of user embedding tends to like this type of item embedding”). The outputs of both parts are combined into a final prediction. This architecture became a standard for high-performance ranking models. 3.3.2 Core Architecture 2: Alibaba’s “Deep Interest Network” (DIN) The Wide & Deep model has a weakness: its “Deep” part compresses the entire user history (e.g., 50 clicked items) into one fixed-size vector. This is wasteful. A user’s interest in a new “hiking boot” is only related to their “backpack” purchase, not their “coffee maker” purchase. Alibaba’s Deep Interest Network (DIN): Key Innovation: Attention. The DIN introduces an attention mechanism — a technique borrowed from NLP. How it works: Instead of averaging the user’s history embeddings, the DIN looks at the candidate item first. It then uses the candidate item’s embedding to “query” the user’s history and calculates attention scores. The “backpack” embedding gets a high attention score. The “coffee maker” embedding gets a low one. The model then computes a weighted average of the user’s history, based on these scores. This creates a context-aware user history vector that is specific to the item being ranked. This was a massive step forward in ranking accuracy for e-commerce. 3.4 Advanced Reco: Context-Aware & In-Session Models This is the new, critical section you requested. 3.4.1 Why Sessions? The “Tyranny of the Long-Term Profile” All the models above are good at modeling a user’s long-term, stable interests. But what about their short-term, dynamic intent? Consider these scenarios: A user who normally buys electronics is suddenly browsing for “baby clothes.” This is a gift-buying mission. You should not pollute their long-term profile and recommend baby clothes for the next six months. A user on Netflix just finished a heavy sci-fi movie. Their immediate intent is likely to find another sci-fi movie, even if their long-term profile shows they mostly watch comedies. This is the goal of In-Session Recommendation: to model the user’s “intent-in-the-moment” based only on the sequence of actions in their current session. 3.4.2 Architectural Shift: From Static Profiles to Dynamic Sequences Classic Model: p(click) = F(User_Profile, Item_Features) In-Session Model: p(next_click) = F( [item_1, item_2, … item_t], Item_Features) The model’s input is not a static profile, but a sequence of the user’s most recent actions (e.g., the last 10 clicks in this session). 3.4.3 Core Architecture 1: RNN/GRU-based (e.g., GRU4Rec) The first major breakthrough in session modeling was using Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs). Model: GRU4Rec (2015) was a seminal paper. How it works: The session is a sequence of item embeddings: [emb(item_A), emb(item_B), emb(item_C)]. This sequence is fed into a GRU (Gated Recurrent Unit), one item at a time. The GRU maintains a “hidden state” vector that acts as a compressed summary of the entire sequence so far. This final “session embedding” (the hidden state after the last item) is then used to predict the next item the user will click. This was the first time a deep learning model could effectively model sequential user behavior. 3.4.4 Core Architecture 2: Transformers (e.g., SASRec) RNNs have a problem: they compress the entire past into one hidden state, which can be a bottleneck. Transformers, with their self-attention mechanism, provide a better solution. Model: SASRec (Self-Attentive Sequential Recommendation) (2018). How it works: The model takes the sequence of item embeddings [emb(A), emb(B), emb(C)] as input. The self-attention mechanism looks at all pairs of items in the sequence. It learns which items are most predictive of the next item. For example, it might learn that if a user clicks (camera_body, tripod), the camera_body is highly predictive of the next click being a (lens), while the tripod is less predictive. It creates a powerful, context-aware summary of the session that outperforms RNNs. 3.4.5 Company Deep Dive: Alibaba & Netflix on In-Session Alibaba (Deep Interest Session Network — DISN): Alibaba took this even further. They recognized that in e-commerce, sessions are very diverse. Their DISN model builds a complex representation of the session, but also combines it with the user’s long-term profile, learning to balance “current intent” vs. “long-term preference.” Netflix (Session-Based Recommendation): Netflix heavily relies on in-session signals. When you finish a show, the recommendations on the “credits” screen are often session-based. They know your immediate context (you just finished this episode/movie) and optimize for that. Their system runs multiple models in parallel: A “long-term” model based on your full history. A “short-term” model based on your last few interactions. A “contextual” model (e.g., “Trending Now,” “New Releases”). The final ranked list is an ensemble of these, giving them the flexibility to capture dynamic intent. 3.5 Solving the Recommendation Cold-Start Problem User Cold Start (New User): Solution: The User Tower falls back to using only context features (location, device_type, time_of_day). This generates a “new user from this location” vector, which will be close to items popular in that region. Item Cold Start (New Item): Solution: This is a major strength of this architecture. The Item Tower generates a high-quality vector based only on content (text and image embeddings). This new item’s vector will be “close” to existing similar items, so it can be recommended immediately. 4.0 Section 2: The E-commerce Search System (The “Fulfillment” Problem) Objective: To fulfill explicit user intent by returning a highly relevant, ranked list of products from a text-based query. 4.1 Defining the Search Funnel Business Goal: Increase search-to-purchase conversion, reduce “zero results” queries, and ensure the most relevant (and profitable) items are at the top. Core Metric: Offline: NDCG (Normalized Discounted Cumulative Gain). This is the king of ranking metrics. It measures ranking quality by heavily rewarding putting the “best” items at the top ranks. Online: Search Conversion Rate, CTR on Top-3 Results. 4.2 Search Stage 1: Retrieval (Candidate Generation) The goal is to find all documents that match the query “blue running shoes.” 4.2.1 Technology 1: Lexical Search (Keyword Matching) Algorithm: BM25 (a successor to TF-IDF). How it works: It uses an Inverted Index (a map from word -> [list of documents]) to find all docs with “blue,” “running,” or “shoes.” Pro: Extremely fast and good at keyword matches. Con: Dumb. It cannot find “sneakers” if the user types “running shoes.” 4.2.2 Technology 2: Semantic Search (Vector Matching) How it works: This is a Two-Tower model, but for query/document text. Query Tower: A BERT-based model that embeds the user’s query (“blue running shoes”) into a query_vector. Document Tower: A BERT-based model that embeds the product_description into a doc_vector. Serving: The doc_vector for all products is indexed in an ANN (Faiss/ScaNN). The query_vector is used to find semantically similar documents. Pro: Understands intent. It can find “sneakers” for the query “running shoes.” 4.2.3 Hybrid Search: The Best of Both The state-of-the-art approach is to run both searches in parallel and combine their scores. Company Deep Dive: Amazon’s Query-Product Embedding System Amazon has published extensive work on this. Their search retrieval is a massive hybrid system. They train models (similar to a Two-Tower) on click and purchase logs to learn a shared embedding space for queries and products. A user searching “quiet laptop fan” (a query) and a user purchasing a “BeQuiet! Silent Wings 120mm” (a product) should map to a similar vector. This allows Amazon to retrieve products that are semantically relevant to the user’s mission, not just textually identical. This is critical for long-tail, descriptive queries. 4.3 Search Stage 2: Re-Ranking via Learning to Rank (LTR) This is the most critical part of search. The ~1000 candidates are not ranked well for business. The BM25 score is just a keyword-matching score. Now, we apply a powerful, feature-rich ML model. Model: LambdaMART. What is it? It is not a neural network. It is a Gradient-Boosted Decision Tree (GBDT) model (like XGBoost or LightGBM). Why? GBDTs are exceptionally powerful for ranking. They are fast at inference, can handle thousands of features, and are not sensitive to un-normalized data. 4.3.1 The LTR Feature Set: The “Secret Sauce” The LambdaMART model is trained on a massive feature set, far richer than the recommendation ranker. Query Features: query_word_count, query_popularity. Document (Item) Features (Query-Independent): These are your business signals. Query-Document Features: bm25_score, semantic_similarity_score, title_match. Company Deep Dive: Amazon’s A9 & Alibaba’s LTR Features The power of Amazon’s A9 search engine is its LTR model, which reportedly uses thousands of features. These are not just text features; they are a mix of: Relevance Features: bm25_score, title_match, semantic_similarity. Popularity Features: sales_rank, clicks_in_last_24h, impressions. Quality/Trust Features: avg_review_score, num_reviews, seller_rating, is_prime_eligible. Economic Features: price, profit_margin, in_stock_status. Alibaba has also published papers on their LTR models, emphasizing that their system heavily models the commercial value of an item, not just its text relevance. The LTR model’s job is to learn the complex trade-off: “This item matches the query perfectly but has 2 stars. This other item is a so-so match but has 5 stars and is on sale. Which should I rank higher?” 4.3.2 Ground Truth: “Judgment Lists” You cannot just train on “clicked=1, not_clicked=0” due to position bias. LTR requires Judgment Lists, which are graded relevance labels for (query, item) pairs. Human-in-the-Loop: (The Gold Standard) Human raters are given a query (e.g., “blue shoes”) and a list of items. They assign a grade: 4 = Perfect, 3 = Excellent, 2 = Good, 1 = Fair, 0 = Bad. Implicit Labels: (Cheaper, but biased) We infer these grades from user behavior: 4 = Purchase, 3 = Add-to-Cart, 2 = Click, 1 = Impression (no click). 4.3.3 The LTR Algorithms: Why LambdaMART Wins Pointwise LTR: A simple regression model to predict the exact grade (e.g., “3”). (Weak) Pairwise LTR: The model is trained on pairs of documents: (query, item_A, item_B). The label is 1 if A is more relevant than B. (Better) Listwise LTR (LambdaMART): Method: This is the state-of-the-art. The model is trained to directly optimize the NDCG metric. The “Lambda” Gradient: This is the key insight. The model uses a special gradient (“Lambda”) for each document. This gradient is weighted by the Delta NDCG — the change in NDCG you would get by moving that document up or down one spot.
Impact: The model learns to focus intensely on getting the top ranks correct. Swapping #1 and #2 (a huge DeltaNDCG) produces a massive training signal. 4.3.4 Advanced LTR: Multi-Objective Ranking (Relevance, Profitability, etc.) A critical realization for any e-commerce business is that search relevance is not the only goal. The business also has competing objectives, such as: Profitability: The business prefers to sell high-margin items. Conversion Rate: The business prefers items that are more likely to be purchased, not just clicked. Stock Level: The business does not want to promote an item that is about to go out-of-stock. Seller Quality: The business wants to promote items from high-rating, reliable third-party sellers. The Problem: What if the most relevant item (a perfect text match) is a low-profit, low-stock item from a 1-star seller? The LTR model, if trained only on relevance, will rank this item at #1, creating a bad user experience and a poor business outcome. The Solution: Multi-Objective Learning to Rank (MO-LTR). This framework trains the model to optimize for a blend of competing goals. The Naive Approach (and Why It’s Wrong) The first-pass idea is to just add profitability as a feature to the LambdaMART model. Why it’s wrong: The model is still optimizing for a single objective: relevance (NDCG based on relevance grades). It will only learn to use the profitability feature if it helps predict relevance. It does not learn the trade-off between relevance and profit. Most importantly, the business has no control over this trade-off. You can't say, "for this query, I want to be 10% more profit-focused." Method 1: Weighted Gain in a Single GBDT (LambdaMART) This is the classic method for “baking in” business objectives into a GBDT model. Modify the “Gain” Metric: The “Lambda” in LambdaMART is calculated based on the change in NDCG (Delta$NDCG). Create a Blended Gain: We redefine the Gain as a weighted combination of our objectives. Train the Model: The LambdaMART model is now trained to optimize the NDCG of this new \text{Blended\_Gain}. The “Business Knob”: The \alpha parameter is a simple scalar (e.g., 0.1) that is set before training. It acts as the “business knob.” The limitation is that this knob is static. You have to re-train the model to change the \alpha value. Method 2: Multi-Task Learning (MTL) in Deep Learning This is the modern, more flexible approach that replaces GBDTs with deep neural networks for ranking. Shared-Bottom Architecture: The model is built with a shared “body” (a DNN) that learns a powerful, combined representation of the (query, item) pair from all the input features. Multiple “Heads”: Instead of one output, the model has multiple output heads that are trained simultaneously: Relevance Head: A softmax classifier predicting the relevance grade (e.g., probabilities for grades 0–4). Profitability Head: A regressor (linear output) predicting the profit. CVR Head: A sigmoid classifier predicting p(\text{Conversion}). p(Stockout) Head: A sigmoid classifier predicting the probability of a stockout. Combined Training Loss: The model’s total loss is a weighted sum of the losses from each head (e.g., L_{\text{total}} = w_1 \cdot L_{\text{cross\_entropy}} + w_2 \cdot L_{\text{MSE}} + w_3 \cdot L_{\text{binary\_cross\_entropy}}). Inference-Time Flexibility (The Real Power): At serving time, the model outputs multiple scores for each item (a relevance score, a profit score, a CVR score). The final ranking score is calculated outside the model using a simple, controllable business-logic formula: \text{Final\_Score} = (w_{\text{rel}} \cdot \text{Relevance}) + (w_{\text{prof}} \cdot \text{Profit}) + (w_{\text{cvr}} \cdot \text{pCVR}) These w weights are not the model’s training weights. They are business-logic knobs that can be changed in real-time without re-training! Company Deep Dive: Alibaba’s & Amazon’s Blended Objectives , Alibaba’s “Entire Space Multi-Task Model” (ESMM): Alibaba is a master of this. Their platform has to balance three goals: user satisfaction (CTR), user purchase (CVR), and merchant advertising revenue (Cost-per-click, CPC). Their MTL models predict all of these simultaneously. The final rank is a blend, ensuring users see relevant items, but also ensuring that merchants who pay for promotion get a fair (but not overwhelming) boost 5.0 Unified MLOps, Evaluation, and The Business Framework While the Recommendation and Search systems are distinct, their operational lifecycle is nearly identical. A model is not a one-time project; it is a continuously evolving product. 5.1 The Feedback Loop: A Continuous System Models go stale. A robust MLOps pipeline is essential. Log: All production interactions (impressions, clicks, searches) are streamed to a message queue (e.g., Kafka). Store: This data is processed in real-time (e.g., Flink) and loaded into a data warehouse (e.g., BigQuery) and a Feature Store. Retrain: Automated training pipelines are triggered on a regular cadence: Search LTR Model: Re-trained daily on the last 30 days of search logs. Reco Ranker Model: Re-trained hourly on the last 72 hours of click logs. Reco Candidate Gen (Two-Tower): Re-trained daily. Deploy: The new model artifacts are versioned and pushed to the serving system. 5.2 Company Deep Dive: MLOps Platforms Uber (Michelangelo): Uber’s MLOps platform, Michelangelo, is a famous example of a system that “closes the loop.” It’s an end-to-end platform that manages data, trains models, evaluates them, and deploys them, all in one place. It allows teams to build and deploy models without reinventing the infrastructure every time. Netflix (Titus): Netflix heavily invested in MLOps, including their container management system Titus, which allows them to run thousands of training and inference jobs at scale. 5.3 Offline Evaluation Reco Stage 1 (Retrieval): Recall@K (e.g., “Did our Top 500 candidates contain the item the user actually bought?”). Reco Stage 2 (Ranking): AUC (How well does it separate clicks from non-clicks?). Search Stage 2 (Ranking): NDCG (How good is the order of the top 10 results?). 5.4 Online Evaluation: The A/B Testing Ground Truth Offline metrics are just proxies. The only test that matters is an online A/B test. Method: Control (Group A): 95% of users see the old model. Treatment (Group B): 5% of users see the new model. Measurement: After 2–4 weeks, do you see a statistically significant lift in the true business KPIs? (e.g., Revenue Per User, Search-to-Purchase Conversion). Company Deep Dive: The A/B Testing Culture Companies like Booking.com, Netflix, and Amazon are famous for their relentless A/B testing culture. Booking.com is known for running thousands of concurrent A/B tests. Nothing gets shipped to production without winning an A/B test against the current champion. Netflix uses A/B tests to validate everything, from new recommendation algorithms to the artwork (thumbnails) they show for a given movie. They discovered that personalizing the artwork can significantly lift engagement.
